<p><b>Gainesville</b> is the <a href="/wiki/County_seat" title="County seat">county seat</a> and largest city in <a href="/wiki/Alachua_County,_Florida" title="Alachua County, Florida">Alachua County, Florida</a>, United States, and the principal city of the <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Gainesville, Florida Metropolitan Statistical Area">Gainesville, Florida, metropolitan statistical area</a>. The US Census Bureau estimated Gainesville's population at 133,857 in 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-USCensusEst2018_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCensusEst2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> a 7.4% increase from 2010. Gainesville is the largest city in the <a href="/wiki/North_Central_Florida" title="North Central Florida">North Central Florida</a> region. The Gainesville metropolitan statistical area had an estimated population of 288,212 in 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville is home to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a>, the nation's <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_United_States_university_campuses_by_enrollment" class="mw-redirect" title="List of largest United States university campuses by enrollment">fifth-largest university campus by enrollment</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Gainesville,_Florida" title="History of Gainesville, Florida">History of Gainesville, Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Gainesville,_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of Gainesville, Florida">Timeline of Gainesville, Florida</a></div>
<p>There is archeological evidence, from about 12,000 years ago, of the presence of <a href="/wiki/Paleo_Indians" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleo Indians">Paleo Indians</a> in the Gainesville area, although it is not known if there were any permanent settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Deptford_culture" title="Deptford culture">Deptford culture</a> campsite existed in Gainesville and was estimated to have been used between 500 BCE and 100 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-histmark1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-histmark1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The Deptford people moved south into Paynes Prairie and Orange Lake during the first century and evolved in to the <a href="/wiki/Cades_Pond_culture" title="Cades Pond culture">Cades Pond culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Milanich_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milanich-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The Deptford people who remained in the Gainesville area were displaced by migrants from southern Georgia sometime in the seventh century.<sup id="cite_ref-Milanich_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milanich-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> These migrants evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Alachua_culture" title="Alachua culture">Alachua culture</a> and they built their burial mound on top of the Deptford culture campsite.<sup id="cite_ref-histmark1_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-histmark1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> When Europeans made first contact in the area, the <a href="/wiki/Potano" title="Potano">Potano</a> lived in the area. They were descendants of the Alachua culture people.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> European contact diminished the numbers of native peoples (through disease, enslavement, war) and Spanish colonists began cattle ranching in the Paynes Prairie area in the 18th century. The Spanish ceded Florida to the US in 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville was established in 1853 and named after <a href="/wiki/Edmund_P._Gaines" title="Edmund P. Gaines">Edmund P. Gaines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The town of Gainesville was incorporated in 1869<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> and chartered as a city in 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The University of the State of Florida was moved from Lake City to Gainesville in 1906 and its name was simplified to <i>University of Florida</i> in 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-britannica_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gainesville is located at 29°39'55" North, 82°20'10" West (29.665245, −82.336097),<sup id="cite_ref-GR1_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GR1-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> which is roughly the same latitude as <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a>, the city has a total area of 62.4 square miles (161.6&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), of which 61.3 square miles (158.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) is land and 1.1 square miles (2.8&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) is water. The total area is 1.74% water.<sup id="cite_ref-Census_2010_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Census_2010-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville's tree canopy is both dense and species rich, including broadleaf evergreens, conifers, and deciduous species; the city has been recognized by the <a href="/wiki/National_Arbor_Day_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="National Arbor Day Foundation">National Arbor Day Foundation</a> every year since 1982 as a "Tree City, USA". A 2016 ecological assessment indicates Gainesville's urban tree canopy covers 47 percent of its land area.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville is the only city with more than 10,000 residents in the <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Gainesville, Florida Metropolitan Statistical Area">Gainesville, Florida, metropolitan statistical area</a> (Alachua and <a href="/wiki/Gilchrist_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Gilchrist County">Gilchrist</a> counties), and it is surrounded by rural area, including the 21,000-acre (8,500&#160;ha) wilderness of <a href="/wiki/Paynes_Prairie" class="mw-redirect" title="Paynes Prairie">Paynes Prairie</a> on its southern edge. The city is characterized by its medium size and central location, about 90 minutes' driving time from either <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a> or <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>, two hours from <a href="/wiki/Tampa,_Florida" title="Tampa, Florida">Tampa</a>, and five hours from either <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> or <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>. The area is dominated by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> which in 2008 was the third-largest university by enrollment in the US,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and as of 2016 was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_university_campuses_by_enrollment" class="mw-redirect" title="List of United States university campuses by enrollment">fifth-largest</a>.
</p>

<div style="height:0em;bottom:2em;width:1.6em;position:absolute;left:0;border-bottom:dotted 1px #abc;padding:0;margin:0">&#160;</div>
<div style="background:#ace;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;color-adjust:exact;position:absolute;bottom:2em;left:.2em;width:1.2em;height:1.20904em;overflow:hidden">&#160;</div>
<div style="color:blue;position:absolute;bottom:.5em;left:0;width:1.6em;height:1.5em;text-align:center"><span style="font-size:70%">60</span></div>
<div style="height:0em;bottom:8em;width:1.6em;position:absolute;left:0;border-bottom:dotted 1px #cba;padding:0;margin:0">&#160;</div>
<div style="overflow:hidden;background:#e44;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;color-adjust:exact;position:absolute;left:.2em;width:1.2em;bottom:9.3733333333333em;height:2.6111111111111em;">&#160;</div>
<div style="color:red;position:absolute;bottom:11.984444444444em; left:-.4em;width:1.6em;height:1.5em;text-align:right"><span style="font-size:80%">20</span></div>
<div style="color:red;position:absolute;bottom:7.8733333333333em; left:-.4em;width:1.6em;height:1.5em;text-align:right"><span style="font-size:80%">7</span></div>
</div></td>
</tr>
<tr><td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: red">Average max. and min. temperatures in °C</td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: blue">Precipitation totals in mm</td></tr></tbody></table>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Gainesville's climate is defined as <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen</a>: Cfa). Due to its inland location, Gainesville experiences wide temperature fluctuations, and it is part of USDA Plant <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zone</a> 9a.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> During the hot season, from roughly May 15 to September 30, the city's climate is similar to the rest of the state, with frequent afternoon thunderstorms and high humidity. Average temperatures range from the low 70s (21–23&#160;°C) at night to around 92&#160;°F (33&#160;°C) during the day.<sup id="cite_ref-Weather_Channel_averages_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weather_Channel_averages-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The all-time record high of 104&#160;°F (40&#160;°C) was reached on June 27, 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-NOWData_JAX_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOWData_JAX-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> From November through March Gainesville experiences a somewhat different climate from much of Florida, with 16 nights<sup id="cite_ref-NOWData_JAX_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOWData_JAX-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> of temperatures at freezing or below and sustained freezes every few years. The all-time record low of 6&#160;°F (−14&#160;°C) was reached on <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1899" title="Great Blizzard of 1899">February 13, 1899</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and the city experienced light snow and freezing rain on Christmas Eve, 1989. Traces of snow were also recorded in 1977,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> 1996, 2010<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The daily average temperature in January is 54.3&#160;°F (12.4&#160;°C); on average, the window for freezing temperatures is December 4 to February 24, allowing a growing season of 282&#160;days.<sup id="cite_ref-NOWData_JAX_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOWData_JAX-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> As with the rest of the state, cold temperatures are almost always accompanied by clear skies and <a href="/wiki/High_pressure_area" class="mw-redirect" title="High pressure area">high pressure systems</a>; snow is therefore rare. Temperatures reaching 100&#160;°F (38&#160;°C) or falling below 20&#160;°F (−7&#160;°C) are rare, having respectively last occurred on June 4, 2019, and January 11, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-NOWData_JAX_26-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOWData_JAX-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The city's flora and fauna are also distinct from coastal regions of the state, and include many deciduous species, such as <a href="/wiki/Dogwood" class="mw-redirect" title="Dogwood">dogwood</a>, maple, hickory and <a href="/wiki/Sweet_gum" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweet gum">sweet gum</a>, alongside palms, <a href="/wiki/Live_oak" title="Live oak">live oaks</a>, and other evergreens. Thus the city enjoys brief periods of fall color in late November and December and a noticeable, prolonged spring from mid-February through early April. This is a generally pleasant period, as colorful blooms of <a href="/wiki/Azalea" title="Azalea">azalea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Redbud" class="mw-redirect" title="Redbud">redbud</a> complement a cloudless blue sky, for this is also the period of the lowest precipitation and lowest humidity. The city averages 47.33 inches (1,200&#160;mm) of rain per year. June through September accounts for a majority of annual rainfall, while autumn and early winter is the driest period.<sup id="cite_ref-Weather_Channel_averages_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weather_Channel_averages-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg/220px-Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg/330px-Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg/440px-Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg 2x" data-file-width="965" data-file-height="1100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_Gainesville_Hippodrome_State_Theater_20050507.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_State_Theatre" title="Hippodrome State Theatre">Hippodrome State Theatre</a></div></div></div>
<p>Since the 1990s, suburban sprawl has been a concern for a majority of the city commissioners. The "New Urbanization" plan to gentrify the area between historic Downtown and the University of Florida may slow the growth of suburban sectors and spark a migration toward upper-level apartments in the inner city. The area immediately north of the university is also seeing active redevelopment. Many gentrification plans rely on tax incentives that have sparked controversy<sup id="cite_ref-Alligator_2007-12-05_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alligator_2007-12-05-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and are sometimes unsuccessful. University Corners, which would not have been proposed without a $98 million tax incentive program by the city,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> was to be "a crowning jewel of the city's redevelopment efforts",<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> 450 condos and hotel units and 98,000 square feet (9,100&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of retail space in eight stories covering three city blocks,<sup id="cite_ref-GVilleSun-2011-01-06_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GVilleSun-2011-01-06-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> on 3.4 acres (1.4&#160;ha) purchased for $15.5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-GVilleSun-2008-07-11_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GVilleSun-2008-07-11-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> 19 thriving businesses<sup id="cite_ref-Alligator_2007-12-05_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alligator_2007-12-05-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> were demolished in April 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-GVilleSun-2008-07-11_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GVilleSun-2008-07-11-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> but in May 2008 deposit checks were refunded to about 105 people who reserved units,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and in July 2008 developers spent "$120,000 to beautify the site, so we won't have this ugly green fence."<sup id="cite_ref-GVilleSun-2008-07-11_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GVilleSun-2008-07-11-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville's east side houses the majority of the city's African-American community, while the west side consists of the mainly student and white resident population. West of the city limits are large-scale planned communities, most notably <a href="/wiki/Haile_Plantation,_Florida" title="Haile Plantation, Florida">Haile Plantation</a>, which was built on the site of its eponymous former plantation.
</p><p>The destruction of the city's landmark <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian</a> courthouse in the 1960s, which some considered unnecessary, brought the idea of historic preservation to the community's attention. The bland county building that replaced the grand courthouse became known to some locals as the "air conditioner". Additional destruction of other historic buildings in the downtown followed. Only a small handful of older buildings are left, like the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_State_Theatre" title="Hippodrome State Theatre">Hippodrome State Theatre</a>, at one time a federal building. Revitalization of the city's core has picked up, and many parking lots and underutilized buildings are being replaced with infill development and near-campus housing that blend in with existing historic structures. There is a proposal to rebuild a replica of the old courthouse on a parking lot one block from the original location.
</p><p>Helping in this effort are the number of areas and buildings added to the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>. Dozens of examples of restored Victorian and <a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_Style_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Anne Style architecture">Queen Anne style</a> residences constructed in the city's agricultural heyday of the 1880s and 1890s can be found in the following districts:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Northeast_Gainesville_Residential_District" title="Northeast Gainesville Residential District">Northeast Gainesville Residential District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Southeast_Gainesville_Residential_District" title="Southeast Gainesville Residential District">Southeast Gainesville Residential District</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pleasant_Street_Historic_District_(Gainesville,_Florida)" title="Pleasant Street Historic District (Gainesville, Florida)">Pleasant Street Historic District</a></li></ul>
<p>Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_Campus_Historic_District" title="University of Florida Campus Historic District">University of Florida Campus Historic District</a>, consisting of 11 buildings and 14 contributing properties, lies within the city's boundaries. Most of the buildings in the Campus Historic District are constructed in variations of <a href="/wiki/Collegiate_Gothic" title="Collegiate Gothic">Collegiate Gothic</a> architecture, which returned to prominence in the late 19th and early 20th centuries.
</p><p>Historic structures on the Register in and around downtown are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bailey_House_(Gainesville,_Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bailey House (Gainesville, Florida)">Bailey Plantation House</a> (1854)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colson_House#Colson_House_(Gainesville,_Florida)" title="Colson House">Colson House</a> (1905)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matheson_House_(Gainesville,_Florida)" title="Matheson House (Gainesville, Florida)">Matheson House</a> (1867)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hotel_Thomas" title="Hotel Thomas">Thomas Hotel</a> (1910)</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Post_Office_(Gainesville,_Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Post Office (Gainesville, Florida)">Old Post Office</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_State_Theatre" title="Hippodrome State Theatre">Hippodrome State Theatre</a>) (1911)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Masonic_Order_Lodge_No._41_(Gainesville,_Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="Masonic Order Lodge No. 41 (Gainesville, Florida)">Masonic Temple</a> (1908)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dixie_Hotel_(Gainesville,_Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dixie Hotel (Gainesville, Florida)">Seagle Building</a> (1926), downtown Gainesville's tallest building.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baird_Hardware_Company_Warehouse" title="Baird Hardware Company Warehouse">Baird Hardware Company Warehouse</a> (1890)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cox_Furniture_Store" title="Cox Furniture Store">Cox Furniture Store</a> (1875)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cox_Furniture_Warehouse" title="Cox Furniture Warehouse">Cox Furniture Warehouse</a> (c. 1890)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epworth_Hall_(Gainesville,_Florida)" title="Epworth Hall (Gainesville, Florida)">Epworth Hall</a> (1884)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_Gainesville_Depot" title="Old Gainesville Depot">Old Gainesville Depot</a> (1907)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mary_Phifer_McKenzie_House" title="Mary Phifer McKenzie House">Mary Phifer McKenzie House</a> (1895)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Star_Garage" title="Star Garage">Star Garage</a> (1902)</li></ul>

<p>The US Census Bureau estimated Gainesville's population at 133,857 in 2018,<sup id="cite_ref-USCensusEst2018_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCensusEst2018-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> a 7.7% increase from 2010. At the 2010 census there were 51,029 households, with 2.2 persons per household. Children under the age of 5 were 4.4% of the population, under 18 13.4%, and people 65 years or over were 8.3% of the population. 64.9% of the population was white, 23.0% black, 6.9% Asian, 0.3% American Indians and Alaska Natives, 0.1% Native Hawaiians and Other Pacific Islanders, 1.9% some other race, and 2.9% reporting two or more races. 10.0% were Hispanic or Latino of any race, and 58.7% were non-Hispanic whites. 51.6% of the population were female. In 2007–11, the estimated median household income was $30,952 and the per capita income was $19,100.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2000, 87.10% of residents spoke English as their first language, while 6.31% spoke Spanish, 1.28% spoke <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, 0.55% spoke French, 0.52% spoke Korean, and 0.50% spoke German as their <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongue" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother tongue">mother tongue</a>. In total, 12.89% of the population spoke languages other than English.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Numerous guides, such as the 2004 <i>Cities Ranked and Rated: More than 400 Metropolitan Areas Evaluated in the U.S. and Canada</i>, have mentioned Gainesville's low cost of living. The restaurants near the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> also tend to be inexpensive. The property taxes are high to offset the cost of the university, as the university's land is tax-exempt, but the median home cost is slightly below the national average, and Gainesville residents, like all Floridians, do not pay state income taxes.
</p><p>The city's job market scored only 6 out of a possible 100 points in the <i>Cities Ranked and Rated</i> guide, as the downside to the low cost of living is an extremely weak local job market that is oversupplied with college-educated residents. The <a href="/wiki/Median_household_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Median household income">median income</a> in Gainesville is slightly below the U.S. average.
</p><p>Gainesville heavily promoted <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> by creating the first <a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff</a> (FIT) in the United States. The FIT allowed small businesses and homeowners to supply electricity into the municipal <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">power grid</a> and paid a premium for the clean, on-site generated solar electricity. The FIT started with a rate of $0.32 per <a href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> and allowed a person or business to enter into a 20-year contract where Gainesville Regional Utilities would purchase the power for 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The FIT ended in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> when the rate was set at $0.18 per kWh, but the city is still seen as a leader in solar power. This increase in solar installations put Gainesville at number 5 in the world in solar installed per capita, beating Japan, France, China and all of the US.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sports drink <a href="/wiki/Gatorade" title="Gatorade">Gatorade</a> was invented in Gainesville in the 1960s as a means of refreshing the UF football team. UF still receives a share of the profits from the beverage, but Gatorade's headquarters are now in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spring_Hill_UF_Health_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spring Hill <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_Health" title="University of Florida Health">UF Health</a> building</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Top_employers">Top employers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Top employers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Century_Tower_(upright2)_(University_of_Florida).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg/170px-Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg/255px-Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg/340px-Century_Tower_%28upright2%29_%28University_of_Florida%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Century_Tower_(upright2)_(University_of_Florida).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Century Tower, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Gainesville urban area is served by <a href="/wiki/Alachua_County_Public_Schools" title="Alachua County Public Schools">Alachua County Public Schools</a>, which has 75 different institutions in the county, most in the Gainesville area. Gainesville is also home to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_College" title="Santa Fe College">Santa Fe College</a>. The University of Florida is a major financial boost to the community, and UF athletic events, including SEC football games, create hundreds of thousands of dollars in additional revenue.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In all, UF contributes nearly $9 billion annually to Florida's economy and is responsible for more than 100,000 jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desegregation">Desegregation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Desegregation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gainesville began <a href="/wiki/School_integration_in_the_United_States" title="School integration in the United States">desegregating its schools</a> in the 1960s. It integrated its high schools from 1968 to 1970. Its black schools were either closed or integrated.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental_research_schools">Developmental research schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Developmental research schools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/P.K._Yonge_Developmental_Research_School" class="mw-redirect" title="P.K. Yonge Developmental Research School">P.K. Yonge Developmental Research School</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="City_libraries">City libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: City libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alachua_County_Library_District" title="Alachua County Library District">Alachua County Library District</a> provides public library service to a county-wide population (in 2013) of 253,451. The Library District has reciprocal borrowing agreements with the surrounding counties of <a href="/wiki/Baker_County,_Florida" title="Baker County, Florida">Baker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bradford_County,_Florida" title="Bradford County, Florida">Bradford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clay_County,_Florida" title="Clay County, Florida">Clay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_County,_Florida" title="Columbia County, Florida">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dixie_County,_Florida" title="Dixie County, Florida">Dixie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilchrist_County,_Florida" title="Gilchrist County, Florida">Gilchrist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lafayette_County,_Florida" title="Lafayette County, Florida">Lafayette</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levy_County,_Florida" title="Levy County, Florida">Levy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marion_County,_Florida" title="Marion County, Florida">Marion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Putnam_County,_Florida" title="Putnam County, Florida">Putnam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Union_County,_Florida" title="Union County, Florida">Union</a>. These agreements are designed to facilitate access to the most conveniently located library facility regardless of an individual's county of residence.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_infrastructure">Government and infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Government and infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2015</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Florida_Department_of_Citrus" title="Florida Department of Citrus">Florida Department of Citrus</a>'s department of economic research is on the UF campus.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="City_government">City government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: City government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gainesville is administered by a 7-member <a href="/wiki/City_commission_government" title="City commission government">city commission</a> (four commissioners are elected by district, two are elected at large, and the mayor is elected at large). The commission and mayoral elections are nonpartisan, with a runoff if there is no majority winner.<sup id="cite_ref-CITY_COMMISIONERS_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CITY_COMMISIONERS-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville's city hall is at 200 E University Avenue.
</p><p>Law enforcement is provided by <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Police_Department" title="Gainesville Police Department">Gainesville Police Department</a>, except for the University of Florida campus which operates the University Police Department.
</p><p>Fire protection within the city limits is provided by the Gainesville Fire Rescue, while the surrounding county is served by the Alachua County Fire Rescue. Ambulance services are provided for the whole county by the Alachua County Fire Rescue.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mayor">Mayor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Mayor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_Gainesville" title="Mayor of Gainesville">Mayor of Gainesville</a></div>
<p>The mayor of Gainesville is the city's official head. Gainesville's citizens elect the mayor to a three-year term, with a two-term maximum.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Since 2016 the mayor has been <a href="/wiki/Lauren_Poe" title="Lauren Poe">Lauren Poe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Homelessness_issues">Homelessness issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Homelessness issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Coalition_for_the_Homeless" title="National Coalition for the Homeless">National Coalition for the Homeless</a> cited Gainesville as the 5th meanest city in the United States for its criminalization of homelessness in the Coalition's two most recent reports (in 2004 and 2009),<sup id="cite_ref-Illegal_to_be_Homeless_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Illegal_to_be_Homeless-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> the latter time for its meal limit ordinance.<sup id="cite_ref-parks_as_soup_kitchens_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parks_as_soup_kitchens-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Gainesville has a number of ordinances targeting the homeless, including an anti-panhandling measure and one prohibiting sleeping outdoors on public property. In 2005, the Alachua Board of County Commissioners and the Gainesville City Commission responded by issuing a written "Ten Year Plan to End Homelessness";<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> which was followed by the 2010 "A Needs Assessment of Unsheltered Homeless Individuals In Gainesville, Florida" presentation to a joint meeting of Gainesville and Alachua County Commissions.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> An indoor homeless shelter was built on the site of the former Gainesville Correctional Institution grounds, with surrounding area designated for tents.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg/220px-Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="89" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg/330px-Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg/440px-Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1288" data-file-height="523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dsg_Gainesville_13th_and_University_Intersection_Approach_20050507.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heading east on University Avenue, approaching 13th Street (<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_441_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 441 in Florida">US 441</a>) intersection</div></div></div>
<p>In 2009, the Gainesville metropolitan statistical area (MSA) ranked seventh highest in the United States in percentage of commuters who biked to work (3.3 percent).<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_roads">Major roads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Major roads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gainesville has an extensive road system, which is served by <a href="/wiki/Interstate_75_in_Florida" title="Interstate 75 in Florida">Interstate 75</a>, and several <a href="/wiki/State_Roads_in_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="State Roads in Florida">Florida State Routes</a>, including State routes <a href="/wiki/State_Road_20_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 20 (Florida)">20</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_Road_24_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 24 (Florida)">24</a>, and <a href="/wiki/State_Road_26_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 26 (Florida)">26</a>. Gainesville is also served by <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_441_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 441 in Florida">US 441</a> and nearby <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_301_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 301 in Florida">US 301</a>, which give a direct route to <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>.
</p>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_county_roads_in_Alachua_County,_Florida" title="List of county roads in Alachua County, Florida">List of county roads in Alachua County, Florida</a></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/File:I-75.svg" class="image"><img alt="I-75.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/23px-I-75.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="23" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/35px-I-75.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/I-75.svg/46px-I-75.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Interstate_75_in_Florida" title="Interstate 75 in Florida">I-75</a> runs northwest and southeast across the western edge of the city, with interchanges at SR 121/SR 331 (exit 382), SR 24 (exit 384), SR 26 (exit 387), and <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_222" title="Florida State Road 222">SR 222</a> (Exit 390).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:US_441.svg" class="image"><img alt="US 441.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/US_441.svg/23px-US_441.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/US_441.svg/35px-US_441.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/US_441.svg/46px-US_441.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_441_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 441 in Florida">US 441</a> is the main local north-south road through Gainesville. It runs on the eastern edge of the University of Florida. It is known to locals as 13th Street, before curving to the northwest and finally joining SR 20, converting into an additional hidden state road. At the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_121" title="Florida State Road 121">SR 121</a>, the <i>DeSoto Trail</i> moves from SR 121 to US 441.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_20.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 20.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Florida_20.svg/23px-Florida_20.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="23" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Florida_20.svg/35px-Florida_20.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Florida_20.svg/46px-Florida_20.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_20" title="Florida State Road 20">SR 20</a> runs northwest and southeast through Gainesville. In east Gainesville, the road again becomes a stand-alone four-lane highway as it heads to <a href="/wiki/Hawthorne,_Florida" title="Hawthorne, Florida">Hawthorne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interlachen,_Florida" title="Interlachen, Florida">Interlachen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Palatka,_Florida" title="Palatka, Florida">Palatka</a>. Northwest of Gainesville, SR 20 coincides with US 441 as a hidden state road through the town of <a href="/wiki/Alachua,_Florida" title="Alachua, Florida">Alachua</a> before splitting at the fork a half-mile from downtown <a href="/wiki/High_Springs,_Florida" title="High Springs, Florida">High Springs</a>. SR 20 then coincides with <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_27_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 27 in Florida">US 27</a> as it heads to <a href="/wiki/Fort_White,_Florida" title="Fort White, Florida">Fort White</a>, <a href="/wiki/Branford,_Florida" title="Branford, Florida">Branford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayo,_Florida" title="Mayo, Florida">Mayo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perry,_Florida" title="Perry, Florida">Perry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tallahassee,_Florida" title="Tallahassee, Florida">Tallahassee</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_24.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 24.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Florida_24.svg/23px-Florida_24.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="23" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Florida_24.svg/35px-Florida_24.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Florida_24.svg/46px-Florida_24.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_24" title="Florida State Road 24">SR 24</a> runs northeast and southwest through Gainesville. The northeast corner of SR 24 and <a href="/wiki/State_Road_222_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 222 (Florida)">SR 222</a> is the site of the <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Regional_Airport" title="Gainesville Regional Airport">Gainesville Regional Airport</a>, before heading to <a href="/wiki/Waldo,_Florida" title="Waldo, Florida">Waldo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starke,_Florida" title="Starke, Florida">Starke</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville</a> (Via.<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_301_in_Florida" title="U.S. Route 301 in Florida">U.S. Route 301</a>)(Gainesville-Jacksonville Highway). Southwest of Gainesville, SR 24 passes through the towns of <a href="/wiki/Archer,_Florida" title="Archer, Florida">Archer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronson,_Florida" title="Bronson, Florida">Bronson</a> before ending at <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Key,_Florida" title="Cedar Key, Florida">Cedar Key</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_26.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 26.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Florida_26.svg/23px-Florida_26.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="23" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Florida_26.svg/35px-Florida_26.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Florida_26.svg/46px-Florida_26.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_26" title="Florida State Road 26">SR 26</a> is the main local east-west road through Gainesville. West of the city, it spans from <a href="/wiki/Fanning_Springs,_Florida" title="Fanning Springs, Florida">Fanning Springs</a> to <a href="/wiki/Trenton,_Florida" title="Trenton, Florida">Trenton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newberry,_Florida" title="Newberry, Florida">Newberry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jonesville,_Florida" title="Jonesville, Florida">Jonesville</a>. Eastward, SR 26 heads to <a href="/wiki/Melrose,_Florida" title="Melrose, Florida">Melrose</a> before reaching its terminus at <a href="/wiki/Putnam_Hall,_Florida" title="Putnam Hall, Florida">Putnam Hall</a> in Putnam County.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_120.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 120.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Florida_120.svg/23px-Florida_120.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Florida_120.svg/35px-Florida_120.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Florida_120.svg/46px-Florida_120.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_120" title="Florida State Road 120">SR 120</a> runs east and west through the city.  Its western end is at the junction with US 441, its eastern end at the junction with SR 24.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_121.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 121.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Florida_121.svg/23px-Florida_121.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Florida_121.svg/35px-Florida_121.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Florida_121.svg/46px-Florida_121.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Road_121" title="Florida State Road 121">SR 121</a> runs north and south on the western part of the city. The DeSoto Trail breaks away as SR 121 heads north to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Butler,_Florida" class="mw-redirect" title="Lake Butler, Florida">Lake Butler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raiford,_Florida" title="Raiford, Florida">Raiford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macclenny,_Florida" title="Macclenny, Florida">Macclenny</a>. Southward, it travels to <a href="/wiki/Williston,_Florida" title="Williston, Florida">Williston</a> before reaching its terminus at Lebanon Station.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_222.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 222.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Florida_222.svg/23px-Florida_222.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Florida_222.svg/35px-Florida_222.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Florida_222.svg/46px-Florida_222.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/State_Road_222_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 222 (Florida)">SR 222</a> runs east and west on the northern part of the city. Its western end of state maintenance is at the junction with I-75 before continuing as County Road 222 to County Road 241, while its eastern end is at the junction with SR 26 a few miles east of the <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Regional_Airport" title="Gainesville Regional Airport">Gainesville Regional Airport</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/File:Florida_331.svg" class="image"><img alt="Florida 331.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Florida_331.svg/23px-Florida_331.svg.png" decoding="async" width="23" height="18" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Florida_331.svg/35px-Florida_331.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Florida_331.svg/46px-Florida_331.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/State_Road_331_(Florida)" class="mw-redirect" title="State Road 331 (Florida)">SR 331</a> runs northeast and southwest through the City. It also serves as a truck route for State Roads 24, 26, and 121.  Despite skirting the Gainesville City Limits, SR&#160;331 runs north and south as a four-lane divided rural highway.</li></ul>
<p>The city's streets lie on a grid system, with four quadrants (NW, NE, SW and SE). All streets are numbered, except for a few major thoroughfares, many of which are named for the towns they lead to (such as <a href="/wiki/Waldo,_Florida" title="Waldo, Florida">Waldo</a> Road (SR 24), <a href="/wiki/Hawthorne,_Florida" title="Hawthorne, Florida">Hawthorne</a> Road (SR 20), <a href="/wiki/Williston,_Florida" title="Williston, Florida">Williston</a> Road (SR 121/SR 331), <a href="/wiki/Archer,_Florida" title="Archer, Florida">Archer</a> Road (also SR 24) and <a href="/wiki/Newberry,_Florida" title="Newberry, Florida">Newberry</a> Road (SR 26)). Streets called Avenues, Places, Roads or Lanes (often remembered by use of the acronym "APRiL") generally run east-west, while other streets generally run north-south.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intercity_rail">Intercity rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Intercity rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amtrak_Thruway_Motorcoach" title="Amtrak Thruway Motorcoach">Amtrak Thruway Motorcoach</a> buses connect with <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_(Amtrak_station)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacksonville (Amtrak station)">Jacksonville (Amtrak station)</a> to the north and <a href="/wiki/Lakeland_(Amtrak_station)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lakeland (Amtrak station)">Lakeland (Amtrak station)</a> to the south. Bus service connects with Amtrak's <a href="/wiki/Silver_Service" title="Silver Service">Silver Service</a>. Amtrak service is available at <a href="/wiki/Palatka_(Amtrak_station)" class="mw-redirect" title="Palatka (Amtrak station)">Palatka</a>, 32 miles (51&#160;km) to the east.
</p><p>At one time, Gainesville had railroad lines extending in six directions and was served by several depots. The earliest route reached the town in 1859. As traffic and business patterns changed, the less heavily used railroads were abandoned beginning in 1943, and some routes realigned, with the last trains running in the middle of Main Street in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Passenger service included different <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Line_Railroad" title="Atlantic Coast Line Railroad">Atlantic Coast Line Railroad</a> (ACL) trains: the <i>Havana Special</i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Champion_(train)" title="Champion (train)">West Coast Champion</a></i> from New York City, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Dixie_Flyer_(train)" title="Dixie Flyer (train)">Dixie Flyer</a></i> from Chicago. Chicago service was furnished by a transfer at Jacksonville to the <i>West Coast Champion</i>. In 1967, the service continued on through <a href="/wiki/Seaboard_Coast_Line_Railroad" title="Seaboard Coast Line Railroad">Seaboard Coast Line Railroad</a> which was created as a merger of ACL and <a href="/wiki/Seaboard_Air_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Seaboard Air Line">Seaboard Air Line</a>. Service into Gainesville had ended by the time of Amtrak's 1971 creation.
</p><p>By the 1980s, the only freight operator into the city was the <a href="/wiki/Seaboard_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Seaboard System">Seaboard System</a> (formerly the Seaboard Coast Line Railroad, now merged into <a href="/wiki/CSX_Transportation" title="CSX Transportation">CSX</a>).
</p>
<h3><span id="Airport.2C_bus.2C_and_others"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Airport,_bus,_and_others">Airport, bus, and others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Airport, bus, and others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to its extensive road network, Gainesville is served by <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Regional_Transit_System" title="Gainesville Regional Transit System">Gainesville Regional Transit System</a>, or RTS, Florida's fourth-largest mass transit system. The area is also served by <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Regional_Airport" title="Gainesville Regional Airport">Gainesville Regional Airport</a> ("GNV"<sup id="cite_ref-airportcodes_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-airportcodes-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>) in the northeast part of the city, with daily service to Atlanta, Dallas-Fort Worth,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Miami, and <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a>.
</p><p>According to the 2000 census, 5.25 percent of Gainesville residents commuted to work by bike, among the highest figures in the nation for a major population center.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gainesville is known for its support of the visual arts. Each year, <a href="/wiki/Gainesville,_Florida#Annual_cultural_events" title="Gainesville, Florida">two large art festivals</a> attract artists and visitors from all over the southeastern United States.
</p><p>Cultural facilities include the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="Florida Museum of Natural History">Florida Museum of Natural History</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harn_Museum_of_Art" class="mw-redirect" title="Harn Museum of Art">Harn Museum of Art</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hippodrome_State_Theatre" title="Hippodrome State Theatre">Hippodrome State Theatre</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Curtis_M._Phillips_Center_for_the_Performing_Arts" title="Curtis M. Phillips Center for the Performing Arts">Curtis M. Phillips Center for the Performing Arts</a>. Smaller theaters include the Acrosstown Repertory Theatre (ART), Actors' Warehouse, and the Gainesville Community Playhouse (GCP). GCP is the oldest community theater group in Florida; in 2006, it christened a new theater building.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The presence of a major university enhances the city's opportunities for cultural lifestyles. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida_College_of_the_Arts" title="University of Florida College of the Arts">University of Florida College of the Arts</a><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> is the umbrella college for the School of Music, School of Theatre and Dance, School of Art and Art History, and a number of other programs and centers including The University Galleries, the Center for World Art, and Digital Worlds. Collectively, the College offers many performance events and artist/lecture opportunities for students and the greater Gainesville community, the majority offered at little or no cost.
</p><p>Since 1989, Gainesville has been home to <a href="/wiki/Theatre_Strike_Force" title="Theatre Strike Force">Theatre Strike Force</a>, the University of Florida's premier <a href="/wiki/Improvisational_theatre" title="Improvisational theatre">improv troupe</a>. Gainesville also hosts several <a href="/wiki/Sketch_comedy" title="Sketch comedy">sketch comedy</a> troupes and <a href="/wiki/Stand-up_comedy" title="Stand-up comedy">stand-up comedians</a>.
</p><p>In April 2003, Gainesville became known as the "Healthiest Community in America" when it won the only "Gold Well City" award given by the Wellness Councils of America (WELCOA).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Headed up by Gainesville Health &amp; Fitness Centers, and with the support of <a href="/wiki/Shands_HealthCare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shands HealthCare">Shands HealthCare</a> and the Gainesville Area Chamber of Commerce, 21 businesses comprising 60 percent of the city's workforce became involved in the "Gold Well City" effort. As of July 2011, Gainesville remained the only city in the country to win the award.
</p><p>The counties surrounding <a href="/wiki/Alachua_County,_Florida" title="Alachua County, Florida">Alachua County</a> vote strongly Republican, while Alachua County votes strongly Democratic.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In the 2008 election, there was a 22% gap in votes in Alachua County between <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_McCain" title="John McCain">John McCain</a>, while the other 11 candidates on the ballot and write-in votes received approximately 1.46% of the vote.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marijuana_culture">Marijuana culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Marijuana culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gainesville is renowned in recreational drug culture for "Gainesville Green", a particularly potent strain of <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">marijuana</a>. <i>Orange and Blue</i> magazine published a feature article in 2003 about the history of Gainesville Green and the local marijuana culture in general.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-1990s, several Gainesville Hemp Festivals took place outside the Alachua County courthouse.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music_scene">Music scene</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Music scene">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Gainesville is well known for its music scene and has spawned a number of bands and musicians, including <a href="/wiki/Tom_Petty_and_the_Heartbreakers" title="Tom Petty and the Heartbreakers">Tom Petty and the Heartbreakers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Stills" title="Stephen Stills">Stephen Stills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Don_Felder" title="Don Felder">Don Felder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Leadon" title="Bernie Leadon">Bernie Leadon</a> of The <a href="/wiki/Eagles_(band)" title="Eagles (band)">Eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Motels" title="The Motels">The Motels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Against_Me!" title="Against Me!">Against Me!</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradley_(singer)" title="Charles Bradley (singer)">Charles Bradley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Less_Than_Jake" title="Less Than Jake">Less Than Jake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hot_Water_Music" title="Hot Water Music">Hot Water Music</a>, Loyal Revival, <a href="/wiki/John_Vanderslice" title="John Vanderslice">John Vanderslice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sister_Hazel" title="Sister Hazel">Sister Hazel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Waters" title="Hundred Waters">Hundred Waters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/For_Squirrels" title="For Squirrels">For Squirrels</a>. It is also the location of independent labels <a href="/wiki/No_Idea_Records" title="No Idea Records">No Idea Records</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elestial_Sound" title="Elestial Sound">Elestial Sound</a>, and the former home of <a href="/wiki/Plan-It-X_Records" title="Plan-It-X Records">Plan-It-X Records</a>, which moved to <a href="/wiki/Bloomington,_Indiana" title="Bloomington, Indiana">Bloomington, Indiana</a>. For two years, the Gainesville nonprofit <a href="/wiki/Harvest_of_Hope_Foundation" title="Harvest of Hope Foundation">Harvest of Hope Foundation</a> hosted the <a href="/wiki/Harvest_of_Hope_Fest" title="Harvest of Hope Fest">Harvest of Hope Fest</a> in St. Augustine.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>  Gainesville is also the home of www.FLAROCKS.com, the founders of "Santa Jam", who hold concerts every December throughout North Florida as a toy fundraiser for sick, injured, and homeless children and a showcase for local musicians. Since 2011 they have distributed nearly 700 toys to hospitals, local churches, homeless charities, and needy families across the area.
</p><p>No Idea Records puts on an annual three-day rock festival known as <a href="/wiki/The_Fest" title="The Fest">The Fest</a>, typically during the last weekend in October, coinciding with the annual Florida-Georgia football game, played in Jacksonville to minimize tensions between the largely out of town music festival goers with the University of Florida students and alumni.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1987 and 1998, Gainesville had a very active rock music scene, with Hollywood star <a href="/wiki/River_Phoenix" title="River Phoenix">River Phoenix</a> having the local club Hardback Cafe as his main base. Phoenix's band <a href="/wiki/Aleka%27s_Attic" title="Aleka&#39;s Attic">Aleka's Attic</a> was a constant feature of the rock scene.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The Phoenix family is still a presence in Gainesville, with <a href="/wiki/Rain_Phoenix" title="Rain Phoenix">Rain Phoenix</a>'s band <a href="/wiki/Papercranes" title="Papercranes">Papercranes</a> and Liberty Phoenix's store, Indigo.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville is still known for its strong music community and was named "Best Place to Start a Band in the United States" by <a href="/wiki/Blender_(magazine)" title="Blender (magazine)"><i>Blender</i> magazine</a> in March 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The article cited the large student population, cheap rent, and friendly venues.
</p><p>Over the past decade, Gainesville has been home to a wide variety of bands, from the <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin American music">Latin</a>/<a href="/wiki/Afrobeat" title="Afrobeat">afrobeat</a> sounds of <a href="/wiki/Umoja_Orchestra" title="Umoja Orchestra">Umoja Orchestra</a>, to the rock of <a href="/wiki/Morningbell" title="Morningbell">Morningbell</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Ska" title="Ska">ska</a> staples <a href="/wiki/The_Know_How" title="The Know How">The Know How</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gainesville's reputation as an independent music mecca can be traced back to 1984 when a local music video station was brought on the air. The station was called TV-69, broadcast on UHF 69 and was owned by Cozzin Communications.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The channel drew considerable media attention thanks to its promotion by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Cosby" title="Bill Cosby">Bill Cosby</a>, who was part owner of the station when it started. TV-69 featured many videos by punk and indie-label bands and had several locally produced videos ("Clone Love" by a local parody band, and a <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur_Jr." title="Dinosaur Jr.">Dinosaur Jr.</a> song).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg/220px-Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg/330px-Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg/440px-Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1728" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ben_Hill_Griffin_Stadium" title="Ben Hill Griffin Stadium">Ben Hill Griffin Stadium</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Florida_Gators" title="Florida Gators">Florida Gators</a> is the varsity team of the University of Florida, competing in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Conference" title="Southeastern Conference">Southeastern Conference</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Collegiate_Athletic_Association" title="National Collegiate Athletic Association">National Collegiate Athletic Association</a> since 1933. It has been ranked in the top 10 in the NACDA ranking since the 1983–84 season. It has won 40 national team championships, including two men's basketball titles, three football titles, four men's golf titles, and seven women's tennis titles.
</p><p>Opened in 1969, the <a href="/wiki/Gainesville_Raceway" title="Gainesville Raceway">Gainesville Raceway</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Dragstrip" title="Dragstrip">dragstrip</a> that hosts the <a href="/wiki/Gatornationals" title="Gatornationals">Gatornationals</a>, one of the four <a href="/wiki/NHRA" class="mw-redirect" title="NHRA">NHRA</a> major races.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Startup_culture">Startup culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Startup culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Roughly since the 2006 founding of <a href="/wiki/Grooveshark" title="Grooveshark">Grooveshark</a>, a Gainesville-based music <a href="/wiki/Streaming_media" title="Streaming media">streaming</a> service, Gainesville has seen an increase in the number of technology-based <a href="/wiki/Startup_companies" class="mw-redirect" title="Startup companies">startup companies</a> founded and developed in the city, particularly the downtown area.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Among them are Digital Brands, SharpSpring, <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(company)" title="Fracture (company)">Fracture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optym" title="Optym">Optym</a>, and Feathr. The city celebrates <a href="/wiki/Josh_Greenberg" title="Josh Greenberg">Josh Greenberg</a> Day annually in April, in honor of the late founder of Grooveshark and his contributions to the community's startup culture.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Annual_cultural_events">Annual cultural events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Annual cultural events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>The Spring Arts Festival, hosted each year, usually in early April, by Santa Fe College (formerly Santa Fe Community College), is one of the three largest annual events in Gainesville and known for its high-quality, unique artwork.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The nationally recognized Downtown Festival and Art Show, hosted each fall by the City of Gainesville, attracts award-winning artists and a crowd of more than 100,000.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The Hoggetowne Medieval Faire has attracted thousands of fairgoers for over 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Fest" title="The Fest">The Fest</a>, a multi-day, multiple-venue underground music festival held annually in Gainesville since 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png/220px-Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="44" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png/330px-Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7a/Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png 2x" data-file-width="406" data-file-height="82" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Independent_Florida_Alligator_logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Independent Florida Alligator Logo</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Print">Print</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Print">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_Florida" title="List of newspapers in Florida">List of newspapers in Florida</a></div>
<p>Gainesville is served by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gainesville_Sun" title="The Gainesville Sun">The Gainesville Sun</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent_Florida_Alligator" title="The Independent Florida Alligator">The Independent Florida Alligator</a></i>, the student newspaper for the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe_College" title="Santa Fe College">Santa Fe College</a>.
</p><p>The New York Times Editing Center also resides in Gainesville.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio">Radio</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Radio">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_radio_stations_in_Florida" title="List of radio stations in Florida">List of radio stations in Florida</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arbitron" class="mw-redirect" title="Arbitron">Arbitron</a> ranks the Gainesville-Ocala market as the nation's 83rd-largest.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Thirteen radio stations are licensed to operate in the city of Gainesville—five AM stations, six commercial FM stations, and two low-power non-commercial FM stations. Three of the stations (<a href="/wiki/WRUF_(AM)" title="WRUF (AM)">WRUF</a>, <a href="/wiki/WRUF-FM" title="WRUF-FM">WRUF-FM</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WUFT-FM" title="WUFT-FM">WUFT-FM</a>) are operated by broadcasting students at the University of Florida. WUFT-FM is the city's <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> member station, while the WRUF stations are operated as commercial stations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_television_stations_in_Florida" title="List of television stations in Florida">List of television stations in Florida</a></div>
<p>Gainesville is the 162nd-largest television market in the nation, as measured by <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_Media_Research" title="Nielsen Media Research">Nielsen Media Research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Broadcast television stations in the Gainesville market include <a href="/wiki/WCJB-TV" title="WCJB-TV">WCJB</a>, an <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>/<a href="/wiki/The_CW_Plus" title="The CW Plus">CW</a> affiliate in Gainesville; <a href="/wiki/WGFL" title="WGFL">WGFL</a>, a <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> affiliate broadcasting from <a href="/wiki/High_Springs,_Florida" title="High Springs, Florida">High Springs</a>; <a href="/wiki/WNBW_(TV)" class="mw-redirect" title="WNBW (TV)">WNBW</a>, a <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> affiliate in Gainesville; <a href="/wiki/WOGX" title="WOGX">WOGX</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> <a href="/wiki/Owned-and-operated_station" title="Owned-and-operated station">owned-and-operated station</a> (O&amp;O) from <a href="/wiki/Ocala,_Florida" title="Ocala, Florida">Ocala</a>; <a href="/wiki/WMYG-LP" class="mw-redirect" title="WMYG-LP">WMYG-LP</a>, an analog <a href="/wiki/MyNetworkTV" title="MyNetworkTV">MyNetworkTV</a> affiliate broadcasting from <a href="/wiki/Lake_City,_Florida" title="Lake City, Florida">Lake City</a>; and <a href="/wiki/WUFT_(TV)" title="WUFT (TV)">WUFT</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Service" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Broadcasting Service">PBS</a> station affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a> in Gainesville.
</p><p>WOGX bears the distinction of being the smallest network O&amp;O in the United States, as a semi-satellite of another Fox O&amp;O, <a href="/wiki/WOFL" title="WOFL">WOFL</a> in <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sister_cities">Sister cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gainesville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Sister cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gainesville maintains <a href="/wiki/Sister_city" title="Sister city">sister city</a> relationships with eight cities:<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Deir_Alla" title="Deir Alla">Deir Alla</a>, Jordan</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Duhok,_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Duhok, Iraq">Duhok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>, Iraq (since 2006)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacmel" title="Jacmel">Jacmel</a>, Haiti</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kfar_Saba" title="Kfar Saba">Kfar Saba</a>, Israel (since 1998)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mejdlaya" title="Mejdlaya">Mejdlaya</a>, Lebanon (since 2015)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matagalpa,_Nicaragua" title="Matagalpa, Nicaragua">Matagalpa, Nicaragua</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Novorossiisk" class="mw-redirect" title="Novorossiisk">Novorossiisk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krasnodar_Krai" title="Krasnodar Krai">Krasnodar Krai</a>, Russia (since 1982)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rzesz%C3%B3w" title="Rzeszów">Rzeszów</a>, <a href="/wiki/Podkarpackie_Voivodeship" title="Podkarpackie Voivodeship">Podkarpackie</a>, Poland (since 2013)</li></ul>
